\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

The outputs of the preprocessing pipeline define the structured input
space for all downstream analyses. In particular, they establish the
feature space (genes/probes) and sample space (annotated tissues and
disease states) upon which both complexity/entropy measures and
latent-space representations are computed. As such, preprocessing is not
merely a technical step but a foundational stage that constrains the
interpretation of all subsequent results.

\section*{Customizing for Other
Datasets}\label{customizing-for-other-datasets}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Customizing for Other Datasets}

\markright{Customizing for Other Datasets}

This pipeline can be adapted to other Affymetrix or GEO datasets by:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  updating the GEO accession and raw-data source;
\item
  adjusting chip-to-platform mappings in \texttt{geo\_chip\_map};
\item
  modifying metadata parsing and label-cleaning logic;
\item
  adding or changing ML export filters; and
\item
  generalizing \texttt{ml\_chip\_id} to a vector of chip IDs if multiple
  platforms should be exported for downstream modeling.
\end{itemize}

See Section: Filtering for formal definition of the feature space used
in downstream complexity and latent-space analyses.

\chapter{Filtering Strategy for the Global Cancer Analysis
Pipeline}\label{filtering-strategy-for-the-global-cancer-analysis-pipeline}

\chapter{Filtering of Microarray Expression
Data}\label{filtering-of-microarray-expression-data}

This document describes the role of probe filtering in the global cancer
analysis workflow. It should be read together with the preprocessing and
analysis pipeline overviews, because filtering in the analysis pipeline
is the stage at which the high-dimensional expression matrix is
converted into comparison-specific feature sets for downstream
complexity, entropy, and biological interpretation tasks.

Latent-space modeling uses a related but distinct feature-selection
strategy defined in the preprocessing pipeline, and therefore does not
necessarily operate on the same comparison-specific feature sets
described here.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Current Analysis
Configuration}\label{current-analysis-configuration}

The current analysis configuration supports two comparison-level
probe-selection regimes, with variance filtering available in two modes:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3000}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.4000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3000}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Method / mode
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedleft
Main parameters
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Primary emphasis
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\texttt{limma} & \texttt{logfc\_cutoff\ =\ 0.33},
\texttt{pval\_cutoff\ =\ 0.05} & Differential-expression-oriented
selection within each predefined normal-tumor comparison \\
\texttt{variance}, \texttt{top\_n} mode &
\texttt{analysis\_top\_n\ =\ 3000} & Fixed-count high-variance probe
selection within each predefined normal-tumor comparison \\
\texttt{variance}, \texttt{threshold} mode &
\texttt{var\_threshold\ =\ 0.75} & Quantile-threshold high-variance
probe selection within each predefined normal-tumor comparison \\
\end{longtable}

In the current configuration, the analysis pipeline uses:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{filter\_method }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"variance"}
\NormalTok{variance\_selection\_mode }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"top\_n"}
\NormalTok{analysis\_top\_n }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{3000}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This comparison-specific \texttt{analysis\_top\_n} setting is distinct
from the global top-N feature-selection strategy used for latent-space
export during preprocessing. Both may use the value 3000, but they
define different feature spaces: the analysis setting defines local,
comparison-specific high-variance probe sets, whereas the latent-space
workflow uses a global chip-level high-variance feature set.

The analysis pipeline applies probe selection in Stage 3, immediately
after expression matrices and normal/tumor comparison maps have been
built. The resulting filtered-probe objects are saved as chip-specific
RDS files:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Output
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Description
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/filtered\_probes/filtered\_probes\_hu35ksuba.rds}
& Filtered probes for \texttt{hu35ksuba} comparisons \\
\texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/filtered\_probes/filtered\_probes\_hu6800.rds}
& Filtered probes for \texttt{hu6800} comparisons \\
\end{longtable}

These files then provide the feature subsets used by the pairwise
complexity and entropy engines.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Why Filtering Is Required}\label{why-filtering-is-required}

Gene-expression microarray datasets are high-dimensional but
sample-limited. The Ramaswamy dataset contains many thousands of probes,
while each tissue-specific normal/tumor comparison contains far fewer
samples. Without filtering, downstream calculations would be dominated
by probes that contribute little stable information: low signal, low
reproducibility, low contrast, inconsistent annotation, or
noise-dominated variation.

Filtering is therefore not only a statistical preprocessing step. In
this project it has three distinct roles:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  it defines the probe space used by traditional complexity and entropy
  measures;
\item
  it provides a comparison-specific feature space that can be contrasted
  with the global feature-selection strategy used for latent-space
  modeling;
\item
  it shapes the biological interpretation of complexity, entropy, and
  latent geometry by determining which genes and gene sets are eligible
  to contribute to the analysis.
\end{enumerate}

These roles are related but not identical. A filter that is appropriate
for differential-expression interpretation is not necessarily identical
to one that stabilizes geometric structure, and neither necessarily
matches the feature-selection regime used for latent-space modeling. For
that reason, filtering should be documented as an analytic choice rather
than treated as a purely mechanical cleanup step.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Filtering and Traditional Complexity/Entropy
Measures}\label{filtering-and-traditional-complexityentropy-measures}

The legacy R-side analysis computes complexity and entropy measures on
normal/tumor expression matrices after comparison-specific probe
selection. In this setting, filtering defines the empirical state space
over which covariance structure, dimensionality, dispersion, and
entropy-like quantities are estimated.

Measures such as effective rank, participation ratio, eigenvalue
entropy, anisotropy, and related covariance-derived statistics are
sensitive to both the number and identity of retained probes. Including
many uninformative or noise-dominated probes can inflate apparent
dimensionality or obscure coherent structure. Conversely, retaining only
highly differential probes can make the analysis more focused but may
bias the measured state space toward large normal/tumor contrasts.

For this reason, the pipeline supports two conceptually distinct
probe-selection regimes:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Filtering mode
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Interpretation for complexity/entropy analysis
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\texttt{limma} & Complexity and entropy are assessed within a
contrast-enriched probe space defined by differential expression between
normal and tumor samples. \\
\texttt{variance} & Complexity and entropy are assessed within a
dispersion-enriched probe space defined by high-variance probes within
each comparison. \\
\end{longtable}

Results obtained under different filtering regimes should not be
interpreted as interchangeable without explicitly considering the effect
of the filtering choice.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Filtering and Latent Space}\label{filtering-and-latent-space}

The latent-space workflow reduces high-dimensional gene-expression data
to a lower-dimensional representation. Filtering affects this workflow
because the model can only learn from the feature set it receives.

In the current project, the latent-space workflow and the R-side
complexity/entropy analysis generally operate on different feature
universes.

The latent-space pipeline uses a fixed, global set of high-variance
probes selected across all samples on a given chip. In contrast, the
R-side analysis uses comparison-specific probe selection based on
predefined normal/tumor contrasts.

As a result, latent-space representations and complexity/entropy
measures are computed in different feature spaces. Direct numerical
correspondence between these systems should therefore be interpreted
cautiously. Agreement may reflect shared biological structure, whereas
disagreement may arise from biological, geometric, or filtering-related
differences.

Latent-space representations should therefore be interpreted as
embeddings of a filtered transcriptomic subspace rather than of the full
gene-expression space.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Filtering and Biological
Meaning}\label{filtering-and-biological-meaning}

Filtering determines the biological vocabulary available for
interpretation. GO terms, KEGG pathways, and other gene-set summaries
depend on which probes remain after filtering and on how those probes
map to annotation resources.

A large retained-probe count in a normal/tumor comparison may indicate
broad transcriptional reorganization, but it can also reflect sample
size, platform coverage, variance structure, or threshold choice. A
small retained-probe count may indicate a more subtle tumor-normal
contrast, but it can also reflect low power, heterogeneous samples,
conservative thresholds, or platform limitations.

Biological interpretation should therefore be phrased cautiously.
Filtered probe sets support statements such as:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  the comparison shows a larger or smaller number of probes meeting the
  configured filter;
\item
  the retained probes are enriched for, or annotated to, particular
  biological functions or pathways;
\item
  complexity/entropy or latent-space patterns are being estimated within
  the retained feature space.
\end{itemize}

They do not by themselves prove that a tissue has globally gained or
lost biological complexity. Rather, they help define the measured
transcriptomic subspace in which complexity, entropy, and latent
geometry are evaluated.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Limma-Oriented Filtering}\label{limma-oriented-filtering}

The \texttt{limma} option is differential-expression-oriented. In the
current configuration, it uses:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{filter\_method }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"limma"}
\NormalTok{logfc\_cutoff  }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FloatTok{0.33}
\NormalTok{pval\_cutoff   }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FloatTok{0.05}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

In this mode, the retained probes are those that pass the configured
normal/tumor contrast criteria. This makes the downstream analysis
strongly tied to normal/tumor transcriptional divergence. It is a
defensible choice when the primary question is how the structure of
expression changes among probes that differ between normal and malignant
tissue.

The corresponding interpretive caution is that the measured complexity
and entropy are conditional on a differential-expression filter. The
results describe the structure of the selected contrast-associated probe
set, not the full transcriptome.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Variance-Oriented Filtering}\label{variance-oriented-filtering}

The \texttt{variance} option is structure-oriented. In the current
configuration, it is parameterized as:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{filter\_method }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"variance"}
\NormalTok{variance\_selection\_mode }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"top\_n"}
\NormalTok{analysis\_top\_n }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{3000}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The variance filter supports two modes. In \texttt{top\_n} mode, a fixed
number of high-variance probes is retained per comparison. In
\texttt{threshold} mode, probes are retained according to the configured
variance threshold:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{var\_threshold }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FloatTok{0.75}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Retained probes are selected based on variability within each
comparison-specific matrix. This can emphasize dominant axes of
variation and support geometric interpretation.

The corresponding interpretive caution is that variance is not
synonymous with biological relevance. Highly variable probes may reflect
tumor biology, tissue identity, cellular mixture, technical effects, or
other sources of heterogeneity. For that reason, variance-filtered
results should be interpreted together with metadata, annotation, and
comparison-specific biological context.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Dynamic Filtering of Gene
Sets}\label{dynamic-filtering-of-gene-sets}

In addition to probe-level filtering, the analysis workflow also uses
gene-set-level filtering for GO, KEGG, MSigDB, or combined gene-set
modes. The current configuration includes:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{gene\_set\_mode   }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"ALL"}
\NormalTok{quantile\_cutoff }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FloatTok{0.75}
\NormalTok{min\_probes      }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{5}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Gene-set filtering is necessary because gene-set sizes are highly
uneven. Some pathways contain many probes, while many GO terms contain
very few mapped probes. Very small gene sets can produce unstable
estimates of complexity, entropy, or enrichment-like summaries. Very
large gene sets can dominate interpretation because they represent broad
biological categories rather than specific mechanisms.

The goal of dynamic filtering is therefore to retain gene sets that are
large enough to support stable calculation while avoiding indiscriminate
inclusion of sparse or poorly supported terms.

\subsection{KEGG pathways}\label{kegg-pathways}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lrr@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Statistic & hu35ksuba & hu6800 \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Median & 18 & 41.5 \\
3rd quartile & 36 & 77.5 \\
Mean & 28.4 & 55.9 \\
Max & 427 & 676 \\
\end{longtable}

Most KEGG pathways contain enough probes to support pathway-level
summaries, although very small or very large pathway mappings still
require caution.

\subsection{GO Molecular Function
terms}\label{go-molecular-function-terms}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lrr@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
Statistic & hu35ksuba & hu6800 \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Median & 2 & 2 \\
3rd quartile & 6 & 6 \\
Mean & \textasciitilde12 & \textasciitilde12 \\
Max & \textasciitilde6400 & \textasciitilde5300 \\
\end{longtable}

Most GO Molecular Function terms are very small in probe-count terms.
This makes minimum-probe thresholds especially important for GO-level
calculations. Without such thresholds, many GO summaries would be based
on too few probes to support stable interpretation.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Application in the Current Analysis
Pipeline}\label{application-in-the-current-analysis-pipeline}

Filtering is implemented as Stage 3 of the analysis workflow. The driver
script sources the grouped filtering wrapper and high-variance helper,
then runs filtering separately for each supported chip. During this
stage, the pipeline validates \texttt{variance\_selection\_mode},
derives the \texttt{top\_n} argument only when variance filtering is run
in \texttt{top\_n} mode, and passes the filtering method,
variance-selection mode, and active parameters to the grouped filtering
wrapper. The resulting RDS objects include provenance metadata
describing the filtering regime used to generate them. The current chips
are:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Chip
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
GEO platform
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Role
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\texttt{hu35ksuba} & \texttt{GPL98} & One Affymetrix platform used in
the Ramaswamy dataset \\
\texttt{hu6800} & \texttt{GPL80} & Second Affymetrix platform used in
the Ramaswamy dataset \\
\end{longtable}

The filtering stage is comparison-aware because it operates against
predefined normal/tumor comparison maps rather than applying one
universal probe list to all comparisons. This is appropriate for the
R-side pairwise framework, where each comparison represents a distinct
empirical contrast.

The practical output of this stage is not a final biological claim. It
is a set of comparison-specific feature lists that define the input
space for subsequent complexity, entropy, aggregation, summary, and
reporting stages.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Filtered Probe Counts}\label{filtered-probe-counts}

The following tables summarize filtered probe counts by tumor category.
These tables are currently included from pre-rendered HTML resources.
That is acceptable for documenting the existing report structure, but
their role in PDF rendering should be reviewed separately when the
reporting interface is audited.

\subsection{Carcinomas}\label{carcinomas}

⚠️ Table not available.

\subsection{Blastomas}\label{blastomas}

⚠️ Table not available.

\subsection{Leukemias}\label{leukemias}

⚠️ Table not available.

\subsection{Lymphomas}\label{lymphomas}

⚠️ Table not available.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Interpreting Filtered Probe
Counts}\label{interpreting-filtered-probe-counts}

Filtered probe counts should be interpreted as properties of a
configured analysis run. They depend on the selected filtering mode,
thresholds, platform, comparison map, and available annotation.

A comparison with many retained probes may reflect broad normal/tumor
transcriptional divergence. It may also reflect larger sample size,
stronger statistical power, or platform-specific probe coverage. A
comparison with few retained probes may reflect a subtler tumor-normal
contrast, heterogeneous samples, conservative thresholds, or technical
constraints.

For hematologic malignancies, high retained-probe counts may be
consistent with strong transcriptional programs related to lineage,
proliferation, immune signaling, or differentiation state. For
epithelial tumors, lower overlap across carcinoma comparisons may
reflect tissue-specific oncogenic programs and differences in cell of
origin. These interpretations are plausible but should remain
conditional on the filtering method and platform.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Shared Probes Within Tumor
Categories}\label{shared-probes-within-tumor-categories}

Shared-probe counts summarize how many filtered probes recur across
comparisons within a broad tumor category. They can be useful for
identifying repeated transcriptional signals within a category, but they
should not be treated as direct measures of biological similarity
without considering tissue composition, platform coverage, and
comparison design.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2727}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3636}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3636}}@{}}
\caption{Number of filtered probes shared across a tumor
category}\tabularnewline
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Tumor category
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedleft
Number of shared probes (\texttt{hu35ksuba})
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedleft
Number of shared probes (\texttt{hu6800})
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endfirsthead
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Tumor category
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedleft
Number of shared probes (\texttt{hu35ksuba})
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedleft
Number of shared probes (\texttt{hu6800})
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Carcinomas & 0 & 0 \\
Blastomas & 51 & 47 \\
Leukemias & 354 & 328 \\
Lymphomas & 181 & NA \\
\end{longtable}

High shared-probe counts suggest that related comparisons may share
recurrent transcriptional features. Low shared-probe counts suggest
either greater biological divergence, stronger tissue specificity,
reduced statistical overlap, or platform-dependent limitations. Missing
values should not be interpreted as absence of signal; they may reflect
absent comparisons or incomplete platform coverage.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section{Relationship to the Analysis Pipeline
Overview}\label{relationship-to-the-analysis-pipeline-overview}

This filtering document corresponds to the current analysis overview in
the following way:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Analysis overview section
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Filtering document connection
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Stage 2: Build expression matrix and comparison maps & Defines the
normal/tumor comparison maps used by comparison-aware filtering \\
Stage 3: Run probe selection & Main implementation point for this
document \\
Stage 4: Run pairwise complexity and entropy comparisons & Consumes the
selected probe sets as feature spaces \\
Stage 5: Aggregate engine results & Aggregates outputs whose values
depend on the selected probe and gene-set filters \\
Stage 6: Summarize comparison-level patterns & Summarizes complexity and
entropy patterns conditional on filtering choices \\
Stage 7-8: Latent workflows & Use a separate global feature-selection
regime defined in preprocessing; this may share the same top-N value but
does not define the same feature space as \texttt{analysis\_top\_n} \\
\end{longtable}

This alignment is important because the same word, ``filtering,'' can
refer to several related but distinct decisions: selecting probes for
pairwise analysis, selecting highly variable features for latent
modeling, and selecting gene sets with enough mapped probes for stable
biological interpretation.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

The empirical structure of filtered probe sets across comparisons is
examined in the Results section, where intersection patterns and
shared-feature distributions are analyzed using UpSet visualizations.

\chapter{Analysis Workflow for Global Cancer Expression
Structure}\label{analysis-workflow-for-global-cancer-expression-structure}

This document describes the analysis pipeline used after preprocessing
of the Ramaswamy global cancer dataset. The pipeline consumes
normalized, metadata-enriched \texttt{ExpressionSet} objects and
full-chip annotation outputs from the preprocessing workflow, constructs
tissue-specific comparison matrices, filters probe sets, runs pairwise
complexity and entropy analyses, aggregates results, summarizes
comparison-level patterns, and optionally executes and postprocesses the
latent-space notebooks.

The purpose of this document is to baseline the current analysis
workflow. It is therefore primarily descriptive. In particular, the
final sections identify the current reporting interface and open
questions about which outputs are stable, which are intermediate, and
which may need further inspection before changing the reporting layer.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section*{Overview}\label{overview-4}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Overview}

\markright{Overview}

\textbf{Driver script:}
\texttt{scripts/analysis/02\_run\_analysis\_pipeline.R}\\
\textbf{Configuration file:}
\texttt{R/config/global\_cancer/analysis\_config.R}

\textbf{Primary upstream inputs:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/global\_cancer\_eset\_list.RData}
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/annotations/full\_chip\_annotations.rds}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Primary analysis outputs:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/expression\_matrices/global\_cancer\_matrix\_maps.RData}
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/filtered\_probes/filtered\_probes\_hu35ksuba.rds}
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/filtered\_probes/filtered\_probes\_hu6800.rds}
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/aggregated/complexity\_aggregated\_results.csv}
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/aggregated/complexity\_aggregated\_results.rds}
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/aggregated/entropy\_aggregated\_results.csv}
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/aggregated/entropy\_aggregated\_results.rds}
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/summaries/summaries\_combined\_df.csv}
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/summaries/summaries\_combined\_df.rds}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Primary latent-space postprocessing outputs:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/tables/latent/}
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/plots/latent/}
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/reports/}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Primary log file:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/logs/analysis/analysis\_pipeline\_log.txt}
\end{itemize}

The analysis pipeline is the main bridge between the preprocessed
expression objects and the downstream reporting layer. It also serves as
the integration point between the older R-side complexity/entropy
workflow and the newer Python/Jupyter latent-space workflow.

These workflows share upstream data but operate on different
feature-selection regimes and therefore different effective feature
spaces.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section*{Historical and Methodological
Context}\label{historical-and-methodological-context-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Historical and Methodological Context}

\markright{Historical and Methodological Context}

The analysis workflow preserves the original dissertation-level aim of
comparing normal and malignant expression structure within
tissue-specific contrasts. In the current implementation, that aim is
operationalized through chip-specific \texttt{ExpressionSet} objects,
predefined tissue/tumor comparison maps, comparison-aware probe
filtering, pairwise complexity and entropy engines, and gene-set-level
annotation.

The pipeline has also been extended to coordinate latent-space analysis.
The latent notebooks are executed from the analysis driver only when
explicitly enabled, and their outputs can then be postprocessed into
tables, plots, and report-facing summary files.

Because the reporting pipeline was developed as a separate abstraction
layer, the analysis workflow should be treated as the place where
reporting inputs originate or are consolidated. Before changing either
pipeline, the practical task is to identify which report-facing objects
are produced here, what formats they use, and whether their formats are
safe to change.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section*{Pipeline Controls}\label{pipeline-controls-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Pipeline Controls}

\markright{Pipeline Controls}

The pipeline is controlled through \texttt{analysis\_config.R}. The
principal toggles are:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Toggle
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Purpose
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\texttt{build\_matrix\_maps} & Build chip-specific tissue matrices and
predefined comparison maps \\
\texttt{run\_filtering} & Run comparison-aware probe selection for each
chip \\
\texttt{run\_pairwise} & Run pairwise complexity and entropy
comparisons \\
\texttt{run\_aggregator} & Aggregate pairwise engine results and attach
gene-set names \\
\texttt{run\_comparison\_summary} & Summarize comparison-level
complexity and entropy patterns \\
\texttt{run\_latent\_notebooks} & Execute the latent-space Jupyter
notebooks through \texttt{nbconvert} \\
\texttt{run\_latent\_postprocessing} & Build latent-space postprocessing
tables, plots, and report-facing outputs \\
\end{longtable}

The current analysis configuration includes:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{filter\_method }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"limma"}  \CommentTok{\# "limma" or "variance"}
\NormalTok{logfc\_cutoff  }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FloatTok{0.33}
\NormalTok{pval\_cutoff   }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FloatTok{0.05}
\NormalTok{var\_threshold }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FloatTok{0.75}
\NormalTok{top\_n         }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{3000} \CommentTok{\# used for latent{-}space export (preprocessing), not comparison{-}level filtering}

\NormalTok{chips   }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"hu35ksuba"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"hu6800"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{engines }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"complexity"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"entropy"}\NormalTok{)}

\NormalTok{gene\_set\_mode   }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"ALL"}
\NormalTok{quantile\_cutoff }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FloatTok{0.75}
\NormalTok{min\_probes      }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{5}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The latent-space postprocessing configuration currently uses:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{latent\_postprocessing\_chip\_id       }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"hu35ksuba"}
\NormalTok{latent\_postprocessing\_mode          }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"ALL"}
\NormalTok{latent\_postprocessing\_gene\_set\_name }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"ALL"}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This means that the R-side complexity/entropy workflow is configured for
both supported chips, while the current latent postprocessing layer is
explicitly focused on \texttt{hu35ksuba}.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section*{Workflow Steps}\label{workflow-steps-1}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Workflow Steps}

\markright{Workflow Steps}

\subsection*{1. Load Configuration and Start
Logging}\label{load-configuration-and-start-logging}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{1. Load Configuration and Start
Logging}

\textbf{Purpose:} Initialize the analysis run, load paths and toggles,
and create the analysis pipeline log.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Configuration: \texttt{R/config/global\_cancer/analysis\_config.R}
\item
  Logger: \texttt{R/helpers/pipeline\_logger.R}
\item
  Log file:
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/logs/analysis/analysis\_pipeline\_log.txt}
\end{itemize}

The driver script begins by sourcing the configuration file into the
global analysis run context. It then starts a pipeline logger and
records the beginning of the run.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsection*{2. Build Expression Matrix and Comparison
Maps}\label{build-expression-matrix-and-comparison-maps}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{2. Build Expression Matrix and
Comparison Maps}

\textbf{Purpose:} Convert preprocessed \texttt{ExpressionSet} objects
into tissue-specific expression matrices and predefined normal/tumor
comparison maps.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Controlled by: \texttt{build\_matrix\_maps}
\item
  Input:
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/global\_cancer\_eset\_list.RData}
\item
  Helper: \texttt{R/helpers/matrix\_builders.R}
\item
  Output:
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/expression\_matrices/global\_cancer\_matrix\_maps.RData}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Behavior:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Load \texttt{eset\_list} from memory if available
\item
  Otherwise load \texttt{eset\_list} from the preprocessing checkpoint
\item
  Build chip-specific matrix lists by tissue
\item
  Define curated comparison maps for each chip, retaining only
  comparisons whose required normal and tumor matrices are present.
\item
  Save the matrix lists and comparison maps as a reusable checkpoint
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Objects saved:}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Object
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Description
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\texttt{matrices\_hu35ksuba} & Tissue-specific expression matrices for
the \texttt{hu35ksuba} chip \\
\texttt{matrices\_hu6800} & Tissue-specific expression matrices for the
\texttt{hu6800} chip \\
\texttt{comparison\_map\_hu35ksuba} & Predefined normal/tumor comparison
map for \texttt{hu35ksuba} \\
\texttt{comparison\_map\_hu6800} & Predefined normal/tumor comparison
map for \texttt{hu6800} \\
\end{longtable}

This stage defines the empirical state spaces used by the later pairwise
comparisons.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsection*{3. Run Comparison-Aware Probe
Selection}\label{run-comparison-aware-probe-selection}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{3. Run Comparison-Aware Probe
Selection}

\textbf{Purpose:} Select probe sets for each predefined normal--tumor
comparison using the configured probe-selection regime, thereby defining
comparison-specific feature spaces for downstream analysis.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Controlled by: \texttt{run\_filtering}
\item
  Loader: \texttt{R/helpers/load\_results\_into\_global.R}
\item
  Wrapper: \texttt{R/wrappers/run\_grouped\_probe\_filtering.R}
\item
  Variance helper: \texttt{R/filters/select\_high\_variance\_probes.R}
\item
  Outputs:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/filtered\_probes/filtered\_probes\_hu35ksuba.rds}
  \item
    \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/filtered\_probes/filtered\_probes\_hu6800.rds}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Current probe-selection regimes:}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2500}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2500}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2500}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.2500}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Regime
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Parameters
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Intended role
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Group sensitivity
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\texttt{limma} & \texttt{logfc\_cutoff}, \texttt{pval\_cutoff} &
Differential-expression-driven feature selection & Explicitly
normal/tumor-aware \\
\texttt{variance}, \texttt{top\_n} mode & \texttt{analysis\_top\_n} &
Fixed-count high-variance feature selection per comparison &
Comparison-aware, not class-label-aware \\
\texttt{variance}, \texttt{threshold} mode & \texttt{var\_threshold} &
Quantile-threshold high-variance feature selection per comparison &
Comparison-aware, not class-label-aware \\
\end{longtable}

In the current configuration, the analysis pipeline uses variance-based
probe selection with:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{filter\_method }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"variance"}
\NormalTok{variance\_selection\_mode }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"top\_n"}
\NormalTok{analysis\_top\_n }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \DecValTok{3000}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The pipeline validates \texttt{variance\_selection\_mode}, derives the
\texttt{top\_n} argument only when
\texttt{filter\_method\ =\ "variance"} and
\texttt{variance\_selection\_mode\ =\ "top\_n"}, and then passes both
\texttt{top\_n} and \texttt{variance\_selection\_mode} into
\texttt{run\_grouped\_probe\_filtering()}. If
\texttt{variance\_selection\_mode\ =\ "threshold"}, \texttt{top\_n} is
set to \texttt{NULL} and \texttt{var\_threshold} is used instead.

The \texttt{analysis\_top\_n} parameter is distinct from the global
top-N feature-selection parameter used for latent-space export during
preprocessing. In the present configuration, both may use the value
3000, but they define different feature spaces:
\texttt{analysis\_top\_n} defines local, comparison-specific
high-variance probe sets, whereas the latent-space export uses a global
chip-level high-variance probe set. The analysis pipeline itself notes
that latent-space workflows may operate on a different feature space
than the comparison-specific probe sets used here.

\textbf{Behavior:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Applies probe selection independently for each predefined comparison
\item
  Uses comparison maps to assemble the relevant normal and tumor
  matrices
\item
  Produces comparison-specific probe sets rather than a single global
  feature space
\item
  Persists selected probe identifiers for reuse in downstream analysis
  and reporting stages
\item
  Records filtering provenance metadata in the filtered-probe result
  object, including chip ID, filtering method, variance-selection mode,
  and active filtering parameters
\end{itemize}

This stage is comparison-aware: probe selection is performed separately
within each predefined tissue-specific normal--tumor contrast. However,
the statistical meaning of ``selected probes'' depends on the configured
regime. The \texttt{limma} regime explicitly uses normal/tumor class
labels, whereas the \texttt{variance} regime selects high-dispersion
probes from the assembled comparison matrix without modeling class
labels directly.

These selected probe sets serve as the input to:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  complexity and entropy calculations in the legacy R framework
\item
  downstream biological interpretation layers, including GO/KEGG
  summaries
\item
  cross-framework comparison with latent-space analyses
\end{itemize}

These probe sets define comparison-specific feature spaces for the
R-side analysis pipeline. In contrast, latent-space workflows typically
operate on a fixed, global feature set selected during preprocessing. As
a result, the two systems may not be evaluated in identical feature
spaces, and comparisons between them should be interpreted accordingly.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsection*{4. Run Pairwise Complexity and Entropy
Comparisons}\label{run-pairwise-complexity-and-entropy-comparisons}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{4. Run Pairwise Complexity and Entropy
Comparisons}

\textbf{Purpose:} Apply the configured analysis engines to each
supported chip-specific normal/tumor comparison.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Controlled by: \texttt{run\_pairwise}
\item
  Loader: \texttt{R/helpers/load\_results\_into\_global.R}
\item
  Annotation helper: \texttt{R/helpers/load\_annotations.R}
\item
  Wrapper: \texttt{R/wrappers/run\_all\_pairwise\_comparisons.R}
\item
  Engines: \texttt{complexity}, \texttt{entropy}
\item
  Gene-set modes: \texttt{ALL}, \texttt{GO\_BP}, \texttt{GO\_MF},
  \texttt{KEGG}, \texttt{MSIGDB}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Inputs:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Matrix/comparison-map checkpoint from Stage 2
\item
  Filtered-probe objects from Stage 3
\item
  Full-chip annotations from preprocessing
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Behavior:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Load expression matrices and comparison maps
\item
  Load filtered probe results
\item
  Load full-chip annotation layer if needed
\item
  Run all pairwise comparisons for configured chips, engines, and
  gene-set modes
\end{itemize}

This stage is where the R-side biological complexity and entropy
analyses are computed. The exact output layout is produced by the
pairwise-comparison wrapper and downstream engine functions; these files
are later collected by the aggregation stage.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsection*{5. Aggregate Engine Results and Attach Gene-Set
Names}\label{aggregate-engine-results-and-attach-gene-set-names}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{5. Aggregate Engine Results and Attach
Gene-Set Names}

\textbf{Purpose:} Consolidate pairwise engine outputs into cleaned
aggregate tables for complexity and entropy.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Controlled by: \texttt{run\_aggregator}
\item
  Loader: \texttt{R/helpers/load\_results\_into\_global.R}
\item
  Aggregator:
  \texttt{R/aggregate/aggregate\_engine\_results\_by\_engine.R}
\item
  Gene-set helper: \texttt{R/helpers/gene\_set\_tools.R}
\item
  Cleaning helper: \texttt{R/helpers/clean\_aggregated\_results.R}
\item
  Outputs:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/aggregated/complexity\_aggregated\_results.csv}
  \item
    \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/aggregated/complexity\_aggregated\_results.rds}
  \item
    \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/aggregated/entropy\_aggregated\_results.csv}
  \item
    \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/aggregated/entropy\_aggregated\_results.rds}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Behavior:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Load pairwise comparison results from the analysis data directory
\item
  Ensure the annotation layer is available
\item
  Aggregate results separately for the complexity and entropy engines
\item
  Attach gene-set names from the annotation layer
\item
  Clean the aggregate tables
\item
  Save both CSV and RDS forms of each aggregate table
\end{itemize}

This stage is a major candidate source for downstream reporting tables
because it produces explicit tabular complexity and entropy result
files.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsection*{6. Summarize Comparison-Level
Patterns}\label{summarize-comparison-level-patterns}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{6. Summarize Comparison-Level
Patterns}

\textbf{Purpose:} Collapse aggregated complexity and entropy outputs
into comparison-level summaries.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Controlled by: \texttt{run\_comparison\_summary}
\item
  Loader: \texttt{R/helpers/load\_results\_into\_global.R}
\item
  Complexity summary: \texttt{R/summarize/summarize\_complexity.R}
\item
  Entropy summary: \texttt{R/summarize/summarize\_entropy.R}
\item
  Summary combiner:
  \texttt{R/summarize/combine\_entropy\_complexity\_summaries.R}
\item
  Outputs:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/summaries/summaries\_combined\_df.csv}
  \item
    \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/summaries/summaries\_combined\_df.rds}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Behavior:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Load cleaned aggregate complexity and entropy results
\item
  Recalculate the complexity summary table
\item
  Recalculate the entropy summary table
\item
  Combine both summaries into a unified comparison-level table
\item
  Save both CSV and RDS forms of the combined summary table
\end{itemize}

This summary file appears to represent one of the most compact R-side
outputs for comparison-level interpretation.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsection*{7. Execute Latent-Space Notebooks
(Optional)}\label{execute-latent-space-notebooks-optional}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{7. Execute Latent-Space Notebooks
(Optional)}

\textbf{Purpose:} Optionally execute the Python/Jupyter latent-space
workflow from the R analysis driver.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Controlled by: \texttt{run\_latent\_notebooks}
\item
  Notebook command:
  \texttt{jupyter\ nbconvert\ -\/-to\ notebook\ -\/-execute}
\item
  Notebook directory: \texttt{projects/cancer-latent-space/notebooks}
\item
  Optional executed-copy directory:
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/notebooks/executed}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Configured notebooks:}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Notebook
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Role
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\texttt{00\_latent\_space\_config.ipynb} & Shared latent-space paths,
configuration, and abbreviations \\
\texttt{01\_load\_and\_inspect\_data.ipynb} & Load and inspect exported
ML inputs \\
\texttt{02\_prepare\_data\_for\_vae.ipynb} & Prepare expression data for
VAE modeling \\
\texttt{03\_train\_first\_vae.ipynb} & Train the initial VAE \\
\texttt{04\_latent\_space\_analysis.ipynb} & Join latent coordinates to
metadata and inspect latent structure \\
\texttt{05\_latent\_complexity\_analysis.ipynb} & Compute
within-comparison latent complexity measures \\
\texttt{06\_between\_cancer\_geometry\_in\_latent\_space\_enhanced.ipynb}
& Compute between-cancer latent geometry measures \\
\end{longtable}

When \texttt{execute\_notebooks\_inplace} is \texttt{TRUE}, notebook
outputs are written back into the notebooks themselves. When
\texttt{FALSE}, executed copies are written to the configured output
directory.

This stage does not itself define the latent-space methodology; it
orchestrates notebook execution from within the R analysis pipeline.

The latent-space workflow operates on feature sets defined during
preprocessing (typically global high-variance probes), which may differ
from the comparison-specific feature spaces used by the R-side analysis
pipeline. As a result, latent-space metrics and R-side
complexity/entropy measures are not necessarily computed on identical
feature spaces.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsection*{8. Run Latent-Space
Postprocessing}\label{run-latent-space-postprocessing}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{8. Run Latent-Space Postprocessing}

\textbf{Purpose:} Build R-side latent-space postprocessing outputs from
the latent notebook results.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Controlled by: \texttt{run\_latent\_postprocessing}
\item
  Script:
  \texttt{R/latent-space/build\_latent\_postprocessing\_outputs.R}
\item
  Table output directory: \texttt{output/global\_cancer/tables/latent/}
\item
  Plot output directory: \texttt{output/global\_cancer/plots/latent/}
\item
  Report output directory: \texttt{output/global\_cancer/reports/}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Current settings:}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{latent\_postprocessing\_chip\_id       }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"hu35ksuba"}
\NormalTok{latent\_postprocessing\_mode          }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"ALL"}
\NormalTok{latent\_postprocessing\_gene\_set\_name }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"ALL"}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This stage is especially relevant to the reporting pipeline because it
appears to produce table, plot, and report-facing outputs after the
Jupyter notebooks have completed. It should be inspected together with
the reporting pipeline before any assumption is made about whether the
current report layer consumes structured files, rendered fragments,
generated QMD content, or a mixture of these.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section*{Reporting Interface and Open
Questions}\label{reporting-interface-and-open-questions}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Reporting Interface and Open Questions}

\markright{Reporting Interface and Open Questions}

The current PDF/HTML rendering issue should be investigated by tracing
report inputs back to the analysis outputs. At this stage, the
appropriate goal is not to prescribe a replacement format, but to
document the existing interface.

Key questions for review:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Question
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Why it matters
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
Which reporting tables are generated directly from
\texttt{complexity\_aggregated\_results.*} and
\texttt{entropy\_aggregated\_results.*}? & These are structured outputs
and may be easier to render consistently across HTML and PDF \\
Which reporting tables are generated from
\texttt{summaries\_combined\_df.*}? & This file may be the cleanest
comparison-level summary source \\
Which reporting tables are produced by latent-space postprocessing? &
The latent postprocessing stage appears to create report-facing
artifacts after notebook execution \\
Are any GO/KEGG/MSigDB tables persisted as rendered HTML rather than
data? & This may explain HTML/PDF rendering divergence, but should be
confirmed before changing code \\
Are generated QMD files reading data frames, CSV/RDS files, HTML
fragments, or preassembled text blocks? & This determines whether the
issue belongs in analysis, latent postprocessing, or reporting \\
Which outputs are relied on by the reporting pipeline exactly as
written? & Some apparent intermediate files may be necessary for the
existing abstraction layer \\
Which output formats can be changed without breaking generated reports?
& This defines the safe scope for future refactoring \\
\end{longtable}

Recommended audit path:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Inspect
  \texttt{R/latent-space/build\_latent\_postprocessing\_outputs.R}.
\item
  Inspect the reporting pipeline for references to
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/aggregated/}.
\item
  Inspect the reporting pipeline for references to
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/summaries/}.
\item
  Inspect the reporting pipeline for references to
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/tables/latent/} and
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/reports/}.
\item
  Identify whether failing GO/KEGG tables originate from aggregate
  CSV/RDS data, generated QMD code, or pre-rendered HTML.
\item
  Only then decide whether changes belong in analysis, latent
  postprocessing, reporting, or the QMD template layer.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section*{Notes for Future
Refactoring}\label{notes-for-future-refactoring}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Notes for Future Refactoring}

\markright{Notes for Future Refactoring}

This baseline does not assume that any particular output format should
be changed. The near-term goal is to trace the data path from analysis
output to report rendering.

Potential later refactoring targets, subject to inspection, include:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  clarifying the distinction between analysis checkpoints and
  report-facing products;
\item
  documenting which aggregate files are canonical for GO, KEGG, and
  MSigDB reporting;
\item
  separating latent notebook execution from latent postprocessing if
  needed;
\item
  standardizing logging sections across analysis and latent
  postprocessing;
\item
  adding explicit validation checks for expected aggregate and summary
  columns;
\item
  documenting which outputs are safe to regenerate independently; and
\item
  identifying whether PDF table failures arise from source data shape,
  generated QMD code, pre-rendered HTML, or LaTeX table-width
  constraints.
\end{itemize}

\chapter{Reporting Workflow for Global Cancer Complexity
Analysis}\label{reporting-workflow-for-global-cancer-complexity-analysis}

This document describes the reporting pipeline used to transform
aggregated complexity, entropy, and pairwise-comparison results into
human-readable summary files, generated comparison-specific Quarto
source files, optional GO-clustering tables, and final rendered report
outputs.

The reporting pipeline is intentionally downstream of the preprocessing
and analysis pipelines. It does not rebuild \texttt{ExpressionSet}
objects, recompute probe annotations, or rerun the core
entropy/complexity calculations. Instead, it loads previously generated
analysis products and converts them into report-ready artifacts.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section*{Overview}\label{overview-5}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Overview}

\markright{Overview}

\textbf{Driver script:}
\texttt{scripts/reporting/03\_run\_report\_pipeline.R}\\
\textbf{Configuration file:}
\texttt{R/config/global\_cancer/report\_config.R}

\textbf{Primary input directories:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/summaries/}
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/aggregated/}
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/filtered\_probes/}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Primary report-output directory:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/reports/}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Primary Quarto/reporting resources:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{quarto/reports/generated\_reports/}
\item
  \texttt{quarto/resources/tables/clusters/}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Primary log file:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/logs/reports/reports\_pipeline\_log.txt}
\end{itemize}

The goal of this reporting pipeline is to create a stable reporting
layer on top of the analysis outputs. Its main responsibilities are to
load previously saved summary and pairwise-comparison objects, generate
cleaned summary artifacts, create comparison-specific Quarto files from
templates, optionally generate GO-clustering tables, and optionally
render the Quarto book/site.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section*{Methodological Context}\label{methodological-context}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Methodological Context}

\markright{Methodological Context}

The reporting layer exists because the analysis pipeline produces
several kinds of intermediate objects that are useful computationally
but not yet suitable for interpretation or documentation. These include
aggregated entropy results, aggregated complexity results, combined
comparison summaries, and filtered-probe objects from both Affymetrix
platforms.

The reporting pipeline therefore performs a translation step:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  from serialized R objects to cleaned report tables;
\item
  from comparison-level results to generated \texttt{.qmd} report files;
\item
  from GO/KEGG-associated analysis outputs to report-ready biological
  tables;
\item
  and, when enabled, from Quarto source files to rendered HTML/PDF
  outputs.
\end{itemize}

This design separates computational analysis from document generation.
That separation is important because rendering a full Quarto book can be
fragile and slow, whereas summary-table generation and \texttt{.qmd}
generation can be tested independently.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section*{Pipeline Controls}\label{pipeline-controls-2}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Pipeline Controls}

\markright{Pipeline Controls}

The reporting pipeline is controlled through \texttt{report\_config.R}.
The principal toggles are:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Toggle
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Purpose
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\texttt{run\_summary\_reports} & Generate text summaries and cleaned
entropy/complexity files \\
\texttt{run\_reports} & Generate comparison-specific \texttt{.qmd} files
from the reporting template \\
\texttt{run\_go\_clustering} & Run GO-clustering analysis and write
cluster tables \\
\texttt{run\_quarto} & Render the Quarto project after report sources
are generated \\
\texttt{run\_visualization} & Placeholder toggle; visualization code is
currently removed from the active pipeline \\
\end{longtable}

The current GO-clustering configuration uses:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{go\_mode }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \StringTok{"GO\_BP"}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Other intended GO modes include \texttt{GO\_MF} and
\texttt{GO\_UNSPECIFIED}, although the validity of a given mode depends
on the downstream clustering script and the available annotation fields.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\section*{Workflow Steps}\label{workflow-steps-2}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Workflow Steps}

\markright{Workflow Steps}

\subsection*{1. Load Reporting Configuration and Start
Logging}\label{load-reporting-configuration-and-start-logging}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{1. Load Reporting Configuration and
Start Logging}

\textbf{Purpose:} Initialize paths, toggles, and the reporting log.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Configuration file: \texttt{R/config/global\_cancer/report\_config.R}
\item
  Logger helper: \texttt{R/helpers/pipeline\_logger.R}
\item
  Log file:
  \texttt{output/global\_cancer/logs/reports/reports\_pipeline\_log.txt}
\end{itemize}

The configuration file defines the study identifier, local output paths,
input RDS paths, and reporting toggles. The driver script then starts a
dedicated reporting log before loading downstream results.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsection*{2. Load Analysis Outputs into the Reporting
Environment}\label{load-analysis-outputs-into-the-reporting-environment}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{2. Load Analysis Outputs into the
Reporting Environment}

\textbf{Purpose:} Load the serialized outputs created by the analysis
pipeline.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Helper script: \texttt{R/helpers/load\_results\_into\_global.R}
\end{itemize}

The driver calls the following helper functions:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{load\_summaries\_df}\NormalTok{(summaries\_dir, }\AttributeTok{overwrite =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{load\_cleaned\_results}\NormalTok{(aggregate\_dir, }\AttributeTok{overwrite =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{load\_filtered\_results}\NormalTok{(filtered\_probes\_dir, }\AttributeTok{overwrite =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

These calls populate the reporting environment with the objects required
by later stages, including:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Object
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Description
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\texttt{summaries\_combined\_df} & Combined pairwise-comparison summary
table \\
\texttt{entropy\_df} & Cleaned/aggregated entropy results \\
\texttt{complexity\_df} & Cleaned/aggregated complexity results \\
\texttt{res\_hu35ksuba} & Filtered-probe results for the
\texttt{hu35ksuba} platform \\
\texttt{res\_hu6800} & Filtered-probe results for the \texttt{hu6800}
platform \\
\end{longtable}

This stage assumes that the analysis pipeline has already written the
required RDS files to \texttt{output/global\_cancer/RData/}.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsection*{3. Generate Summary
Reports}\label{generate-summary-reports}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{3. Generate Summary Reports}

\textbf{Purpose:} Create text summaries and cleaned report-level copies
of the entropy and complexity result tables.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Controlled by: \texttt{run\_summary\_reports}
\item
  Script: \texttt{R/reports/summarize\_pairwise\_results.R}
\end{itemize}

The pipeline summarizes entropy and complexity results separately:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{entropy\_report    }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{summarize\_pairwise\_results}\NormalTok{(entropy\_df, }\AttributeTok{engine =} \StringTok{"entropy"}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{complexity\_report }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{summarize\_pairwise\_results}\NormalTok{(complexity\_df, }\AttributeTok{engine =} \StringTok{"complexity"}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\textbf{Outputs:}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 2\tabcolsep) * \real{0.5000}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
File
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Description
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\texttt{output/global\_cancer/reports/entropy\_summary.txt} & Plain-text
entropy summary \\
\texttt{output/global\_cancer/reports/complexity\_summary.txt} &
Plain-text complexity summary \\
\texttt{output/global\_cancer/reports/entropy\_cleaned.csv} & CSV copy
of cleaned entropy results \\
\texttt{output/global\_cancer/reports/complexity\_cleaned.csv} & CSV
copy of cleaned complexity results \\
\texttt{output/global\_cancer/reports/entropy\_cleaned.rds} & RDS copy
of cleaned entropy results \\
\texttt{output/global\_cancer/reports/complexity\_cleaned.rds} & RDS
copy of cleaned complexity results \\
\end{longtable}

The current driver also renders:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{R}\SpecialCharTok{/}\NormalTok{reports}\SpecialCharTok{/}\NormalTok{pairwise\_summary\_report.Rmd}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This appears to be a legacy or parallel R Markdown summary report. It
should be retained unless and until the Quarto reporting layer fully
replaces it.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsection*{4. Generate Comparison-Specific Quarto
Files}\label{generate-comparison-specific-quarto-files}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{4. Generate Comparison-Specific Quarto
Files}

\textbf{Purpose:} Create one \texttt{.qmd} file per pairwise comparison
from a report template.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Controlled by: \texttt{run\_reports}
\item
  Wrapper: \texttt{R/wrappers/run\_all\_reports.R}
\end{itemize}

The driver passes the major reporting objects into
\texttt{run\_all\_reports()}:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{run\_all\_reports}\NormalTok{(}
  \AttributeTok{summaries\_combined\_df =}\NormalTok{ summaries\_combined\_df,}
  \AttributeTok{complexity\_df =}\NormalTok{ complexity\_df,}
  \AttributeTok{entropy\_df =}\NormalTok{ entropy\_df,}
  \AttributeTok{res\_hu35ksuba =}\NormalTok{ res\_hu35ksuba,}
  \AttributeTok{res\_hu6800 =}\NormalTok{ res\_hu6800}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This stage is responsible for generating comparison-level Quarto source
files, not for rendering the final book. The source files are expected
to be consumed later by the Quarto project under \texttt{quarto/}.

\textbf{Conceptual output:}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  comparison-specific \texttt{.qmd} files, typically under
  \texttt{quarto/reports/generated\_reports/}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Important distinction:}\\
Despite the name \texttt{run\_reports}, this stage primarily creates
report source files. The actual Quarto rendering step is controlled
separately by \texttt{run\_quarto}.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist

\item

\item
  Current output base: \texttt{quarto/resources/tables/clusters/}
\item

\end{itemize}

The driver calls:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

  \AttributeTok{complexity\_df =}\NormalTok{ complexity\_df,}
  \AttributeTok{entropy\_df =}\NormalTok{ entropy\_df,}
  \AttributeTok{output\_dir\_base =}\NormalTok{ here}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{here}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"quarto"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"resources"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"tables"}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{"clusters"}\NormalTok{),}
  \AttributeTok{go\_mode =}\NormalTok{ go\_mode}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This stage is currently disabled by default in
\texttt{report\_config.R}:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{run\_go\_clustering }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \ConstantTok{FALSE}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

When enabled, it should be treated as a biological-interpretation
support layer, not as a replacement for the primary entropy and
complexity summaries.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\subsection*{7. Render the Quarto
Project}\label{render-the-quarto-project}
\addcontentsline{toc}{subsection}{7. Render the Quarto Project}

\textbf{Purpose:} Render the Quarto book/site from the generated source
files.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Controlled by: \texttt{run\_quarto}
\item
  Working directory during render: \texttt{quarto/}
\end{itemize}

When enabled, the driver temporarily changes into the Quarto directory,
removes Quarto's \texttt{\_freeze} directory, and renders the project:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{old }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{getwd}\NormalTok{()}
\FunctionTok{setwd}\NormalTok{(here}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{here}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"quarto"}\NormalTok{))}
\FunctionTok{unlink}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"\_freeze"}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{recursive =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{force =} \ConstantTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{quarto}\SpecialCharTok{::}\FunctionTok{quarto\_render}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{"."}\NormalTok{)}
\FunctionTok{setwd}\NormalTok{(old)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

The current configuration disables this step by default:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{run\_quarto }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \ConstantTok{FALSE}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This is a conservative default. It allows the pipeline to regenerate
source files and summary tables without forcing a full Quarto render on
every reporting run.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}
